<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png/300px-Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png" width="300" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png/450px-Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png/600px-Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png 2x" data-file-width="1769" data-file-height="977" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Structure of a vanadium <a href="/wiki/Porphyrin" title="Porphyrin">porphyrin</a> compound (left) extracted from petroleum by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Treibs" title="Alfred E. Treibs">Alfred E. Treibs</a>, father of <a href="/wiki/Organic_geochemistry" title="Organic geochemistry">organic geochemistry</a>. Treibs noted the close structural similarity of this molecule and <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll_a" title="Chlorophyll a">chlorophyll a</a> (right).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Petroleum is a <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> derived from ancient <a href="/wiki/Fossilized" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossilized">fossilized</a> <a href="/wiki/Organic_material" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic material">organic materials</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Vast quantities of these remains settled to sea or lake bottoms, mixing with sediments and being buried under <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_sea_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Anoxic sea water">anoxic conditions</a>. As further layers settled to the sea or lake bed, intense heat and pressure build up in the lower regions. This process caused the organic matter to change, first into a waxy material known as <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>, which is found in various <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shales</a> around the world, and then with more heat into liquid and gaseous hydrocarbons via a process known as <a href="/wiki/Catagenesis_(geology)" title="Catagenesis (geology)">catagenesis</a>. Formation of petroleum occurs from hydrocarbon <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> in a variety of mainly <a href="/wiki/Endothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> reactions at high temperature and/or pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were certain warm nutrient-rich environments such as the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> and the ancient <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Tethys Sea">Tethys Sea</a> where the large amounts of organic material falling to the ocean floor exceeded the rate at which it could decompose. This resulted in large masses of organic material being buried under subsequent deposits such as shale formed from mud. This massive organic deposit later became heated and transformed under pressure into oil.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Geologists often refer to the temperature range in which oil forms as an "oil window"<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>—below the minimum temperature oil remains trapped in the form of kerogen, and above the maximum temperature the oil is converted to natural gas through the process of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_cracking" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal cracking">thermal cracking</a>. Sometimes, oil formed at extreme depths may migrate and become trapped at a much shallower level. The <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_Oil_Sands" class="mw-redirect" title="Athabasca Oil Sands">Athabasca Oil Sands</a> are one example of this.</p>
<p>An alternative mechanism was proposed by Russian scientists in the mid-1850s, the hypothesis of <a href="/wiki/Abiogenic_petroleum_origin" title="Abiogenic petroleum origin">abiogenic petroleum origin</a>, but this is contradicted by geological and <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">geochemical</a> evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-glasby2006_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-glasby2006-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abiogenic (formed by inorganic means) sources of oil have been found, but never in commercially profitable amounts. The controversy isn't over whether abiogenic oil reserves exist, said Larry Nation of the American Association of Petroleum Geologists. The controversy is over how much they contribute to Earth's overall reserves and how much time and effort geologists should devote to seeking them out.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reservoirs">Reservoirs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reservoirs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crude_oil_reservoirs">Crude oil reservoirs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Crude oil reservoirs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(October 2016)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Structural_Trap_(Anticlinal).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg/220px-Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg.png" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg/330px-Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg/440px-Structural_Trap_%28Anticlinal%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="413" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Structural_Trap_(Anticlinal).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Hydrocarbon trap</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Three conditions must be present for oil reservoirs to form: a <a href="/wiki/Source_rock" title="Source rock">source rock</a> rich in hydrocarbon material buried deep enough for subterranean heat to cook it into oil, a <a href="/wiki/Porous" class="mw-redirect" title="Porous">porous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Permeability_(fluid)" class="mw-redirect" title="Permeability (fluid)">permeable</a> reservoir rock for it to accumulate in, and a cap rock (seal) or other mechanism that prevents it from escaping to the surface. Within these reservoirs, fluids will typically organize themselves like a three-layer cake with a layer of water below the oil layer and a layer of gas above it, although the different layers vary in size between reservoirs. Because most hydrocarbons are less dense than rock or water, they often migrate upward through adjacent rock layers until either reaching the surface or becoming trapped within porous rocks (known as <a href="/wiki/Oil_reservoir" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil reservoir">reservoirs</a>) by impermeable rocks above. However, the process is influenced by underground water flows, causing oil to migrate hundreds of kilometres horizontally or even short distances downward before becoming trapped in a reservoir. When hydrocarbons are concentrated in a trap, an <a href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil field</a> forms, from which the liquid can be extracted by <a href="/wiki/Drill" title="Drill">drilling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pump" title="Pump">pumping</a>.</p>
<p>The reactions that produce oil and natural gas are often modeled as first order breakdown reactions, where hydrocarbons are broken down to oil and natural gas by a set of parallel reactions, and oil eventually breaks down to natural gas by another set of reactions. The latter set is regularly used in <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemical</a> plants and <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refineries</a>.</p>
<p>Wells are drilled into oil reservoirs to extract the crude oil. "Natural lift" production methods that rely on the natural reservoir pressure to force the oil to the surface are usually sufficient for a while after reservoirs are first tapped. In some reservoirs, such as in the Middle East, the natural pressure is sufficient over a long time. The natural pressure in most reservoirs, however, eventually dissipates. Then the oil must be extracted using "<a href="/wiki/Artificial_lift" title="Artificial lift">artificial lift</a>" means. Over time, these "primary" methods become less effective and "secondary" production methods may be used. A common secondary method is <a href="/wiki/Water_injection_(oil_production)" title="Water injection (oil production)">"waterflood"</a> or injection of water into the reservoir to increase pressure and force the oil to the drilled shaft or "wellbore." Eventually "tertiary" or "enhanced" oil recovery methods may be used to increase the oil's flow characteristics by injecting steam, carbon dioxide and other gases or chemicals into the reservoir. In the United States, primary production methods account for less than 40 percent of the oil produced on a daily basis, secondary methods account for about half, and tertiary recovery the remaining 10 percent. Extracting oil (or "bitumen") from oil/tar sand and oil shale deposits requires mining the sand or shale and heating it in a vessel or retort, or using "in-situ" methods of injecting heated liquids into the deposit and then pumping out the oil-saturated liquid.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unconventional_oil_reservoirs">Unconventional oil reservoirs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Unconventional oil reservoirs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">Unconventional oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">Oil sands</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale_reserves" title="Oil shale reserves">Oil shale reserves</a></div>
<p>Oil-eating bacteria <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegrade</a> oil that has escaped to the surface. <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">Oil sands</a> are reservoirs of partially biodegraded oil still in the process of escaping and being biodegraded, but they contain so much migrating oil that, although most of it has escaped, vast amounts are still present—more than can be found in conventional oil reservoirs. The lighter fractions of the crude oil are destroyed first, resulting in reservoirs containing an extremely heavy form of crude oil, called crude bitumen in Canada, or extra-heavy crude oil in <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>. These two countries have the world's largest deposits of oil sands.</p>
<p>On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Oil_shales" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil shales">oil shales</a> are source rocks that have not been exposed to heat or pressure long enough to convert their trapped hydrocarbons into crude oil. Technically speaking, oil shales are not always shales and do not contain oil, but are fined-grain sedimentary rocks containing an insoluble organic solid called <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>. The kerogen in the rock can be converted into crude oil using heat and pressure to simulate natural processes. The method has been known for centuries and was patented in 1694 under British Crown Patent No. 330 covering, "A way to extract and make great quantities of pitch, tar, and oil out of a sort of stone." Although oil shales are found in many countries, the United States has the world's largest deposits.<sup id="cite_ref-Lambertson_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lambertson-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crudes.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Crudes.PNG/350px-Crudes.PNG" width="350" height="384" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Crudes.PNG 1.5x" data-file-width="459" data-file-height="504" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crudes.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Some <a href="/wiki/Benchmark_(crude_oil)" title="Benchmark (crude oil)">marker crudes</a> with their <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> content (horizontal) and <a href="/wiki/API_gravity" title="API gravity">API gravity</a> (vertical) and relative production quantity</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Benchmark_(crude_oil)" title="Benchmark (crude oil)">Benchmark (crude oil)</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">petroleum industry</a> generally classifies crude oil by the geographic location it is produced in (e.g. <a href="/wiki/West_Texas_Intermediate" title="West Texas Intermediate">West Texas Intermediate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brent_oilfield" title="Brent oilfield">Brent</a>, or <a href="/wiki/DME_Oman_Crude_Oil_Futures_Contract" title="DME Oman Crude Oil Futures Contract">Oman</a>), its <a href="/wiki/API_gravity" title="API gravity">API gravity</a> (an oil industry measure of density), and its sulfur content. Crude oil may be considered <i><a href="/wiki/Light_crude_oil" title="Light crude oil">light</a></i> if it has low density or <i><a href="/wiki/Heavy_crude_oil" title="Heavy crude oil">heavy</a></i> if it has high density; and it may be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Sweet_crude_oil" title="Sweet crude oil">sweet</a> if it contains relatively little sulfur or <i><a href="/wiki/Sour_crude_oil" title="Sour crude oil">sour</a></i> if it contains substantial amounts of sulfur.</p>
<p>The geographic location is important because it affects transportation costs to the refinery. <i>Light</i> crude oil is more desirable than <i>heavy</i> oil since it produces a higher yield of gasoline, while <i>sweet</i> oil commands a higher price than <i>sour</i> oil because it has fewer environmental problems and requires less refining to meet sulfur standards imposed on fuels in consuming countries. Each crude oil has unique molecular characteristics which are revealed by the use of <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil_assay" title="Crude oil assay">Crude oil assay</a> analysis in petroleum laboratories.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)" title="Barrel (unit)">Barrels</a> from an area in which the crude oil's molecular characteristics have been determined and the oil has been classified are used as pricing <a href="/wiki/Benchmark_(crude_oil)" title="Benchmark (crude oil)">references</a> throughout the world. Some of the common reference crudes are:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/West_Texas_Intermediate" title="West Texas Intermediate">West Texas Intermediate</a> (WTI), a very high-quality, sweet, light oil delivered at <a href="/wiki/Cushing,_Oklahoma" title="Cushing, Oklahoma">Cushing, Oklahoma</a> for North American oil</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brent_Crude" title="Brent Crude">Brent Blend</a>, consisting of 15 oils from fields in the <a href="/wiki/Brent_oilfield" title="Brent oilfield">Brent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ninian" class="mw-redirect" title="Ninian">Ninian</a> systems in the <a href="/wiki/East_Shetland_Basin" title="East Shetland Basin">East Shetland Basin</a> of the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. The oil is landed at <a href="/wiki/Sullom_Voe" title="Sullom Voe">Sullom Voe</a> terminal in <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>. Oil production from Europe, Africa and Middle Eastern oil flowing West tends to be priced off this oil, which forms a <a href="/wiki/Benchmark_(crude_oil)" title="Benchmark (crude oil)">benchmark</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dubai_Crude" title="Dubai Crude">Dubai-Oman</a>, used as benchmark for Middle East sour crude oil flowing to the Asia-Pacific region</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tapis_crude" title="Tapis crude">Tapis</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, used as a reference for light Far East oil)</li>
<li>Minas (from <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, used as a reference for heavy Far East oil)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/OPEC_Reference_Basket" title="OPEC Reference Basket">OPEC Reference Basket</a>, a weighted average of oil blends from various <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> (The Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries) countries</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Midway-Sunset_Oil_Field" title="Midway-Sunset Oil Field">Midway Sunset</a> Heavy, by which heavy oil in California is priced<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Western_Canadian_Select" title="Western Canadian Select">Western Canadian Select</a> the benchmark crude oil for emerging heavy, high TAN (acidic) crudes.<sup id="cite_ref-NRCPetroleumProducts_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NRCPetroleumProducts-64">[64]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>There are declining amounts of these benchmark oils being produced each year, so other oils are more commonly what is actually delivered. While the reference price may be for West Texas Intermediate delivered at Cushing, the actual oil being traded may be a discounted Canadian heavy oil—Western Canadian Select— delivered at <a href="/wiki/Hardisty,_Alberta" class="mw-redirect" title="Hardisty, Alberta">Hardisty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, and for a Brent Blend delivered at Shetland, it may be a discounted Russian Export Blend delivered at the port of <a href="/wiki/Primorsk,_Leningrad_Oblast" title="Primorsk, Leningrad Oblast">Primorsk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Petroleum_industry">Petroleum industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Petroleum industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This article needs to be <b>updated</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(April 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">Petroleum industry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png/300px-2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png" width="300" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png/450px-2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png/600px-2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png 2x" data-file-width="753" data-file-height="586" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Crude oil export treemap (2012) from Harvard Atlas of Economic Complexity<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg/220px-Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg.png" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg/330px-Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg/440px-Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="548" data-file-height="391" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crude_oil_price_WTI_EIA_since_2000.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/New_York_Mercantile_Exchange" title="New York Mercantile Exchange">New York Mercantile Exchange</a> prices ($/bbl) for West Texas Intermediate 2000 through Oct 2014</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The petroleum industry is involved in the global processes of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon_exploration" title="Hydrocarbon exploration">exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum" title="Extraction of petroleum">extraction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">refining</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_transport" title="Petroleum transport">transporting</a> (often with <a href="/wiki/Oil_tanker" title="Oil tanker">oil tankers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a>), and marketing petroleum products. The largest volume products of the industry are <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">fuel oil</a> and gasoline. Petroleum is also the raw material for many <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">chemical products</a>, including pharmaceuticals, solvents, fertilizers, pesticides, and plastics. The industry is usually divided into three major components: <a href="/wiki/Upstream_(oil_industry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Upstream (oil industry)">upstream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midstream" title="Midstream">midstream</a> and <a href="/wiki/Downstream_(oil_industry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Downstream (oil industry)">downstream</a>. Midstream operations are usually included in the downstream category.</p>
<p>Petroleum is vital to many industries, and is of importance to the maintenance of industrialized <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> itself, and thus is a critical concern to many nations. Oil accounts for a large percentage of the world's energy consumption, ranging from a low of 32 percent for Europe and Asia, up to a high of 53 percent for the Middle East, South and Central America (44%), Africa (41%), and North America (40%). The world at large consumes 30 billion <a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)" title="Barrel (unit)">barrels</a> (4.8&#160;km³) of oil per year, and the top oil consumers largely consist of developed nations. In fact, 24 percent of the oil consumed in 2004 went to the United States alone,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> though by 2007 this had dropped to 21 percent of world oil consumed.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the US, in the states of <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, California, Hawaii, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Western_States_Petroleum_Association" title="Western States Petroleum Association">Western States Petroleum Association</a> (WSPA) represents companies responsible for producing, distributing, refining, transporting and marketing petroleum. This non-profit trade association was founded in 1907, and is the oldest petroleum trade association in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipping">Shipping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Shipping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1950s, shipping costs made up 33 percent of the price of oil transported from the Persian Gulf to USA,<sup id="cite_ref-economist.com_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist.com-70">[70]</a></sup> but due to the development of <a href="/wiki/Supertankers" class="mw-redirect" title="Supertankers">supertankers</a> in the 1970s, the cost of shipping dropped to only 5 percent of the price of Persian oil in USA.<sup id="cite_ref-economist.com_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist.com-70">[70]</a></sup> Due to the increase of the value of the crude oil during the last 30 years, the share of the shipping cost on the final cost of the delivered commodity was less than 3% in 2010. For example, in 2010 the shipping cost from the Persian Gulf to the USA was in the range of 20 $/t and the cost of the delivered crude oil around 800 $/t.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Price">Price</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Price">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png/220px-Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png/330px-Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png/440px-Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png 2x" data-file-width="1764" data-file-height="1155" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crude_oil_prices_since_1861.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nominal and inflation-adjusted US dollar price of crude oil, 1861–2015</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Price_of_oil" title="Price of oil">Price of oil</a></div>
<p>After the collapse of the OPEC-administered pricing system in 1985, and a short-lived experiment with netback pricing, oil-exporting countries adopted a market-linked pricing mechanism.<sup id="cite_ref-Mabro_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mabro-71">[71]</a></sup> First adopted by <a href="/wiki/PEMEX" class="mw-redirect" title="PEMEX">PEMEX</a> in 1986, market-linked pricing was widely accepted, and by 1988 became and still is the main method for pricing crude oil in international trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Mabro_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mabro-71">[71]</a></sup> The current reference, or pricing markers, are <a href="/wiki/Brent_Crude" title="Brent Crude">Brent</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Texas_Intermediate" title="West Texas Intermediate">WTI</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dubai_Crude" title="Dubai Crude">Dubai/Oman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mabro_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mabro-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_product" title="Petroleum product">Petroleum product</a></div>
<p>The chemical structure of petroleum is <a href="/wiki/Heterogeneity" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterogeneity">heterogeneous</a>, composed of hydrocarbon chains of different lengths. Because of this, petroleum may be taken to <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refineries</a> and the hydrocarbon chemicals separated by <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> and treated by other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_process" title="Chemical process">chemical processes</a>, to be used for a variety of purposes. The total cost of a plant is about 9 billion dollars per plant.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuels">Fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ride_with_hitler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ride_with_hitler.jpg/220px-Ride_with_hitler.jpg" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ride_with_hitler.jpg/330px-Ride_with_hitler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/Ride_with_hitler.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="464" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ride_with_hitler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A poster used to promote <a href="/wiki/Carpooling" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpooling">carpooling</a> as a way to ration gasoline during World War II</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most common <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">distillation fractions</a> of petroleum are <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuels</a>. Fuels include (by increasing boiling temperature range):<sup id="cite_ref-Speight1_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speight1-72">[72]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_derivatives">Other derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Certain types of resultant hydrocarbons may be mixed with other non-hydrocarbons, to create other end products:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alkenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkenes">Alkenes</a> (olefins), which can be manufactured into plastics or other compounds</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">Lubricants</a> (produces light machine oils, <a href="/wiki/Motor_oil" title="Motor oil">motor oils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grease_(lubricant)" title="Grease (lubricant)">greases</a>, adding <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> stabilizers as required)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">Wax</a>, used in the packaging of <a href="/wiki/Frozen_food" title="Frozen food">frozen foods</a>, among others</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">Sulfur</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>. These are useful industrial materials. Sulfuric acid is usually prepared as the acid precursor <a href="/wiki/Oleum" title="Oleum">oleum</a>, a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Hydrodesulfurization" title="Hydrodesulfurization">sulfur removal</a> from fuels.</li>
<li>Bulk <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">tar</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">Asphalt</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Petroleum_coke" title="Petroleum coke">Petroleum coke</a>, used in speciality carbon products or as solid fuel</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">Paraffin wax</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aromatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aromatic">Aromatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemicals</a> to be used as precursors in other <a href="/wiki/Chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical">chemical</a> production</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the 1940s, agricultural productivity has increased dramatically, due largely to the increased use of energy-intensive <a href="/wiki/Mechanization" title="Mechanization">mechanization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Petroleum_by_country">Petroleum by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Petroleum by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption">Consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the US Energy Information Administration (EIA) estimate for 2011, the world consumes 87.421 million barrels of oil each day.</p>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OilConsumptionpercapita.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/OilConsumptionpercapita.png/750px-OilConsumptionpercapita.png" width="750" height="347" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/OilConsumptionpercapita.png/1125px-OilConsumptionpercapita.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/OilConsumptionpercapita.png 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="628" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OilConsumptionpercapita.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oil consumption per capita (darker colors represent more consumption, gray represents no data) <i>(source: see file description)</i>

<p>In petroleum industry parlance, <i>production</i> refers to the quantity of crude extracted from reserves, not the literal creation of the product.</p>

<p><sup>1</sup> Peak production of conventional oil already passed in this state</p>
<p><sup>2</sup> Although Canada's conventional oil production is declining, its total oil production is increasing as oil sands production grows. When oil sands are included, Canada has the world's second largest oil reserves after Saudi Arabia.</p>
<p><sup>3</sup> Though still a member, Iraq has not been included in production figures since 1998</p>
<p><sup>4</sup> Trinidad and Tobago has the worlds third largest pitch lake situated La Brea south Trinidad</p>
<p>In 2013, the United States will produce an average of 11.4 million barrels a day, which would make it the second largest producer of hydrocarbons,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and is expected to overtake Saudi Arabia before 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Export">Export</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Export">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Environmental effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_petroleum_industry" title="Environmental impact of the petroleum industry">Environmental impact of the petroleum industry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dieselrainbow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Dieselrainbow.jpg/220px-Dieselrainbow.jpg" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Dieselrainbow.jpg/330px-Dieselrainbow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Dieselrainbow.jpg/440px-Dieselrainbow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="976" data-file-height="871" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dieselrainbow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diesel fuel spill on a road</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because petroleum is a naturally occurring substance, its presence in the environment need not be the result of human causes such as accidents and routine activities (<a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismic</a> exploration, <a href="/wiki/Boring_(earth)" title="Boring (earth)">drilling</a>, extraction, refining and combustion). Phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_seep" title="Petroleum seep">seeps</a><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tar_pit" title="Tar pit">tar pits</a> are examples of areas that petroleum affects without man's involvement. Regardless of source, petroleum's effects when released into the environment are similar.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_acidification">Ocean acidification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Ocean acidification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg/220px-Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg.png" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg/330px-Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg/440px-Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="334" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbonate_system_of_seawater.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Seawater acidification</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">Ocean acidification</a> is the increase in the acidity of the Earth's oceans caused by the uptake of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) from the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. This increase in acidity inhibits all marine life - having a greater impact on smaller organisms as well as shelled organisms (see <a href="/wiki/Scallops" class="mw-redirect" title="Scallops">scallops</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_warming">Global warming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Global warming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When burned, petroleum releases carbon dioxide, a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>. Along with the burning of coal, petroleum combustion may be the largest contributor to the increase in atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> has risen over the last 150 years to current levels of over 390&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">ppmv</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere#Past_variation" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">180 – 300&#160;ppmv of the prior 800 thousand years</a><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This rise in temperature may have reduced the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_ice_pack" title="Arctic ice pack">Arctic ice cap</a> to 1,100,000&#160;sq&#160;mi (2,800,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, smaller than ever recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Because of this melt, more oil reserves have been revealed. It is estimated by the International Energy Agency that about 13 percent of the world's undiscovered oil resides in the Arctic.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraction">Extraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Extraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Oil extraction is simply the removal of oil from the reservoir (oil pool). Oil is often recovered as a water-in-oil emulsion, and <a href="/wiki/Specialty_chemicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Specialty chemicals">specialty chemicals</a> called <a href="/wiki/Demulsifiers" class="mw-redirect" title="Demulsifiers">demulsifiers</a> are used to separate the oil from water. Oil extraction is costly and sometimes environmentally damaging. Offshore exploration and extraction of oil disturbs the surrounding marine environment.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_spills">Oil spills</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Oil spills">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">Oil spill</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_oil_spills" title="List of oil spills">List of oil spills</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil-spill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Oil-spill.jpg/230px-Oil-spill.jpg" width="230" height="230" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Oil-spill.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="240" data-file-height="240" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Kelp after an oil spill</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_Slick_in_the_Timor_Sea_September-2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Oil_Slick_in_the_Timor_Sea_September-2009.jpg/230px-Oil_Slick_in_the_Timor_Sea_September-2009.jpg" width="230" height="115" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Oil_Slick_in_the_Timor_Sea_September-2009.jpg/345px-Oil_Slick_in_the_Timor_Sea_September-2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Oil_Slick_in_the_Timor_Sea_September-2009.jpg/460px-Oil_Slick_in_the_Timor_Sea_September-2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4448" data-file-height="2224" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Oil slick from the <a href="/wiki/Montara_oil_spill" title="Montara oil spill">Montara oil spill</a> in the Timor Sea, September, 2009</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:PrestigeVolunteersInGaliciaCoast.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/PrestigeVolunteersInGaliciaCoast.jpg/230px-PrestigeVolunteersInGaliciaCoast.jpg" width="230" height="158" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/PrestigeVolunteersInGaliciaCoast.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="289" data-file-height="199" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Volunteers cleaning up the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Prestige_oil_spill" title="Prestige oil spill">Prestige oil spill</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Crude oil and refined fuel <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">spills</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tanker_(ship)" title="Tanker (ship)">tanker ship</a> accidents have damaged natural <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Galapagos_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Galapagos Islands">Galapagos Islands</a>, France and many <a href="/wiki/List_of_oil_spills" title="List of oil spills">other places</a>.</p>
<p>The quantity of oil spilled during accidents has ranged from a few hundred tons to several hundred thousand tons (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Bp_oil_spill" class="mw-redirect" title="Bp oil spill">Deepwater Horizon Oil Spill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Empress" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic Empress">Atlantic Empress</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amoco_Cadiz" title="Amoco Cadiz">Amoco Cadiz</a>). Smaller spills have already proven to have a great impact on ecosystems, such as the <a href="/wiki/Exxon_Valdez_oil_spill" title="Exxon Valdez oil spill">Exxon Valdez oil spill</a></p>
<p>Oil spills at sea are generally much more damaging than those on land, since they can spread for hundreds of nautical miles in a thin <a href="/wiki/Oil_slick" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil slick">oil slick</a> which can cover beaches with a thin coating of oil. This can kill sea birds, mammals, shellfish and other organisms it coats. Oil spills on land are more readily containable if a makeshift earth dam can be rapidly <a href="/wiki/Bulldozed" class="mw-redirect" title="Bulldozed">bulldozed</a> around the spill site before most of the oil escapes, and land animals can avoid the oil more easily.</p>
<p>Control of oil spills is difficult, requires ad hoc methods, and often a large amount of manpower. The dropping of bombs and incendiary devices from aircraft on the <a href="/wiki/SS_Torrey_Canyon" title="SS Torrey Canyon">SS&#160;<i>Torrey Canyon</i></a> wreck produced poor results;<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> modern techniques would include pumping the oil from the wreck, like in the <a href="/wiki/Prestige_oil_spill" title="Prestige oil spill">Prestige oil spill</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Erika_(tanker)" class="mw-redirect" title="Erika (tanker)">Erika</a> oil spill.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though crude oil is predominantly composed of various hydrocarbons, certain nitrogen heterocylic compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Pyridine" title="Pyridine">pyridine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Picoline" title="Picoline">picoline</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quinoline" title="Quinoline">quinoline</a> are reported as contaminants associated with crude oil, as well as facilities processing oil shale or coal, and have also been found at legacy <a href="/wiki/Creosote" title="Creosote">wood treatment</a> sites. These compounds have a very high water solubility, and thus tend to dissolve and move with water. Certain naturally occurring bacteria, such as <a href="/wiki/Micrococcus" title="Micrococcus">Micrococcus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthrobacter" title="Arthrobacter">Arthrobacter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rhodococcus" title="Rhodococcus">Rhodococcus</a> have been shown to degrade these contaminants.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tarballs">Tarballs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Tarballs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A tarball is a blob of crude oil (not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">tar</a>, which is a man-made product derived from pine trees or refined from petroleum) which has been weathered after floating in the ocean. Tarballs are an aquatic <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutant</a> in most environments, although they can occur naturally, for example in the Santa Barbara Channel of California<sup id="cite_ref-itah_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-itah-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hostettler_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hostettler-95">[95]</a></sup> or in the Gulf of Mexico off Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Their concentration and features have been used to assess the extent of <a href="/wiki/Oil_spills" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil spills">oil spills</a>. Their composition can be used to identify their sources of origin,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> and tarballs themselves may be dispersed over long distances by deep sea currents.<sup id="cite_ref-hostettler_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hostettler-95">[95]</a></sup> They are slowly decomposed by bacteria, including <i><a href="/wiki/Chromobacterium_violaceum" title="Chromobacterium violaceum">Chromobacterium violaceum</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cladosporium_resinae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cladosporium resinae (page does not exist)">Cladosporium resinae</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bacillus_submarinus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bacillus submarinus (page does not exist)">Bacillus submarinus</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrococcus_varians&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Micrococcus varians (page does not exist)">Micrococcus varians</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_aeruginosa" title="Pseudomonas aeruginosa">Pseudomonas aeruginosa</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Candida_marina&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Candida marina (page does not exist)">Candida marina</a></i> and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saccharomyces_estuari&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Saccharomyces estuari (page does not exist)">Saccharomyces estuari</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-itah_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-itah-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Whales">Whales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Whales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>James S. Robbins has argued that the advent of petroleum-refined kerosene saved some species of great whales from <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> by providing an inexpensive substitute for <a href="/wiki/Whale_oil" title="Whale oil">whale oil</a>, thus eliminating the economic imperative for open-boat <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_petroleum">Alternatives to petroleum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Alternatives to petroleum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a></div>
<p>In the United States in 2007 about 70 percent of petroleum was used for transportation (e.g. gasoline, diesel, jet fuel), 24 percent by industry (e.g. production of plastics), 5 percent for residential and commercial uses, and 2 percent for electricity production.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Outside of the US, a higher proportion of petroleum tends to be used for electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_petroleum-based_vehicle_fuels">Alternatives to petroleum-based vehicle fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Alternatives to petroleum-based vehicle fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel_vehicle" title="Alternative fuel vehicle">Alternative fuel vehicle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">Hydrogen economy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Green_vehicle" title="Green vehicle">Green vehicle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg/220px-Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg/330px-Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg/440px-Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Piracicaba_10_2008_151_Gast_station_selling_four_fuels.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazilian</a> fuel station with four <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative fuels</a> for sale: <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> (B3), <a href="/wiki/Gasohol" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasohol">gasohol</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gasohol#E20.2C_E25" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasohol">E25</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">neat ethanol</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gasohol#E100" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasohol">E100</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> (CNG)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alternative fuel vehicles refers to both:</p>
<ul>
<li>Vehicles that use <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative fuels</a> used in standard or modified <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicle" title="Natural gas vehicle">natural gas vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neat_ethanol_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Neat ethanol vehicle">neat ethanol vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>-powered vehicles, propane autogas, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_internal_combustion_engine_vehicle" title="Hydrogen internal combustion engine vehicle">hydrogen vehicles</a>.</li>
<li>Vehicles with advanced propulsion systems that reduce or substitute petroleum use such as <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">battery electric vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid_electric_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Plug-in hybrid electric vehicle">plug-in hybrid electric vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_electric_vehicle" title="Hybrid electric vehicle">hybrid electric vehicles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_vehicle" title="Hydrogen vehicle">hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell_vehicle" title="Fuel cell vehicle">fuel cell vehicles</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_using_oil_in_industry">Alternatives to using oil in industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Alternatives to using oil in industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Biological feedstocks do exist for industrial uses such as <a href="/wiki/Bioplastic" title="Bioplastic">Bioplastic</a> production.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Hydroelectric energy could also be used instead of gasoline for cars, reducing the amount of oil needed for the world. Another, is the electric powered cars currently in production.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_burning_petroleum_for_electricity">Alternatives to burning petroleum for electricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Alternatives to burning petroleum for electricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Alternative_energy" title="Alternative energy">Alternative energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">Nuclear power</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a></div>
<p>In oil producing countries with little refinery capacity, oil is sometimes burned to produce electricity. <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a> technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Micro_hydro" title="Micro hydro">micro hydro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> are used, but the primary alternatives remain large-scale <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear</a> and coal-fired generation.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_of_petroleum_production">Future of petroleum production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Future of petroleum production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/220px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/330px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/440px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="540" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US oil production and imports, 1910-2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Consumption in the twentieth and twenty-first centuries has been abundantly pushed by automobile growth; the <a href="/wiki/1980s_oil_glut" title="1980s oil glut">1985–2003 oil glut</a> even fueled the sales of low economy vehicles in <a href="/wiki/OECD" class="mw-redirect" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries. The 2008 economic crisis seems to have had some impact on the sales of such vehicles; still, the 2008 oil consumption shows a small increase. In 2016 however Goldman Sachs predicts lower demand due to emerging economies concerns, especially China.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BRICS" title="BRICS">BRICS</a> (Brasil, Russia, India, China, South Africa) countries might also kick in, as China briefly was the first automobile market in December 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The immediate outlook still hints upwards. In the long term, uncertainties linger; the <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> believes that the OECD countries will push low consumption policies at some point in the future; when that happens, it will definitely curb oil sales, and both OPEC and <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">EIA</a> kept lowering their 2020 consumption estimates during the past 5 years.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Oil products are more and more in competition with alternative sources, mainly coal and natural gas, both cheaper sources. Production will also face an increasingly complex situation; while OPEC countries still have large reserves at low production prices, newly found reservoirs often lead to higher prices; offshore giants such as <a href="/wiki/Tupi_oil_field" class="mw-redirect" title="Tupi oil field">Tupi</a>, Guara and <a href="/wiki/Tiber_oilfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiber oilfield">Tiber</a> demand high investments and ever-increasing technological abilities. Subsalt reservoirs such as Tupi were unknown in the twentieth century, mainly because the industry was unable to probe them. <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_Oil_Recovery" class="mw-redirect" title="Enhanced Oil Recovery">Enhanced Oil Recovery</a> (EOR) techniques (example: <a href="/wiki/Daqing_Field" class="mw-redirect" title="Daqing Field">DaQing</a>, China<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> ) will continue to play a major role in increasing the world's recoverable oil. The reach of available petroleum ressources has always been around 35 years or even less since the start of the modern exploration. The <a href="/wiki/Oil_constant" title="Oil constant">oil constant</a>, a insider pun in the German industry refers to that effect.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peak_oil">Peak oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Peak oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">Peak oil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg/300px-GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg" width="300" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg/450px-GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b3/GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="432" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GlobalPeakOilForecast.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global peak oil forecast</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Peak oil is the projection that future petroleum production (whether for individual oil wells, entire oil fields, whole countries, or worldwide production) will eventually peak and then decline at a similar rate to the rate of increase before the peak as these reserves are exhausted. The peak of oil discoveries was in 1965, and oil production per year has surpassed oil discoveries every year since 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> However, this does not mean that potential oil production has surpassed oil demand.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Marion_King_Hubbert" class="mw-redirect" title="Marion King Hubbert">Hubbert</a> applied his theory to accurately predict the peak of U.S. conventional oil production at a date between 1966 and 1970. This prediction was based on data available at the time of his publication in 1956. In the same paper, Hubbert predicts world peak oil in "half a century" after his publication, which would be 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is difficult to predict the oil peak in any given region, due to the lack of knowledge and/or transparency in accounting of global oil reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Based on available production data, proponents have previously predicted the peak for the world to be in years 1989, 1995, or 1995–2000. Some of these predictions date from before the recession of the early 1980s, and the consequent reduction in global consumption, the effect of which was to delay the date of any peak by several years. Just as the 1971 U.S. peak in oil production was only clearly recognized after the fact, a peak in world production will be difficult to discern until production clearly drops off.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The peak is also a moving target as it is now measured as "liquids", which includes synthetic fuels, instead of just conventional oil.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> (IEA) said in 2010 that production of conventional crude oil had peaked in 2006 at 70 MBBL/d, then flattened at 68 or 69 thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Since virtually all economic sectors rely heavily on petroleum, <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>, if it were to occur, could lead to a "partial or complete failure of markets".<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In the mid-2000s, widespread fears of an imminent peak led to the "peak oil movement," in which over one hundred thousand Americans prepared, individually and collectively, for the "post-carbon" future.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unconventional_production">Unconventional production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Unconventional production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The calculus for peak oil has changed with the introduction of unconventional production methods. In particular, the combination of <a href="/wiki/Directional_drilling" title="Directional drilling">horizontal drilling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> has resulted in a significant increase in production from previously uneconomic plays.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Analysts expect that $150 billion will be spent on further developing North American tight oil fields in 2015. The large increase in tight oil production is one of the reasons behind the price drop in late 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Certain rock <a href="/wiki/Strata" class="mw-redirect" title="Strata">strata</a> contain hydrocarbons but have low permeability and are not thick from a vertical perspective. Conventional vertical wells would be unable to economically retrieve these hydrocarbons. Horizontal drilling, extending horizontally through the strata, permits the well to access a much greater volume of the strata. Hydraulic fracturing creates greater permeability and increases hydrocarbon flow to the wellbore.</p>

<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Schneider-Mayerson Matthew (2015). Peak Oil: Apocalyptic Environmentalism and Libertarian Political Culture. University of Chicago Press. <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780226285436" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-226-28543-6</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.eia.gov/analysis/petroleum/crudetypes/pdf/crudetypes.pdf"><i>U.S. Crude Oil Production Forecast- Analysis of Crude Types</i></a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>, Washington, DC: U.S. Energy Information Administration, 29 May 2014<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">31 May</span> 2014</span>, <q>U.S. oil production has grown rapidly in recent years. U.S. Energy Information Administration (EIA) data, which reflect combined production of crude oil and lease condensate, show a rise from 5.7 million barrels per day (bbl/d) in 2011 to 7.4 million bbl/d in 2013. EIA's <i>Short-Term Energy Outlook (STEO)</i> projects continuing rapid production growth in 2014 and 2015, with forecast production in 2015 reaching 9.2 million bbl/d. Beyond 2015, EIA's <i>Annual Energy Outlook (AEO)</i> projects further production growth, although its pace and duration remain uncertain. Domestic production plateaus near 9.6 million bbl/d between 2017 and 2020, close to its historic high of 9.6 million bbl/d in 1970, in the AEO2014 Reference case. In the AEO2014 High Oil and Gas Resource case, growth continues through the 2020s and into the 2030s, with production reaching 13.3 million barrels per day in 2036.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APetroleum&amp;rft.btitle=U.S.+Crude+Oil+Production+Forecast-+Analysis+of+Crude+Types&amp;rft.date=2014-05-29&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.eia.gov%2Fanalysis%2Fpetroleum%2Fcrudetypes%2Fpdf%2Fcrudetypes.pdf&amp;rft.place=Washington%2C+DC&amp;rft.pub=U.S.+Energy+Information+Administration&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ovale, Peder. "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.tu.no/petroleum/2014/12/11/her-ser-du-hvorfor-oljeprisen-faller">Her ser du hvorfor oljeprisen faller</a>" <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://translate.google.dk/translate?sl=da&amp;tl=en&amp;js=y&amp;prev=_t&amp;hl=da&amp;ie=UTF-8&amp;u=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.tu.no%2Fpetroleum%2F2014%2F12%2F11%2Fher-ser-du-hvorfor-oljeprisen-faller&amp;edit-text=">In English</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Teknisk_Ukeblad" title="Teknisk Ukeblad">Teknisk Ukeblad</a></i>, 11 December 2014.
